Bequest of Ring and Its Gem: Applying 'Amm and Khass
Fiqh example illustrating 'amm and khass equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of 'amm and khass equality to resolve conflicts in bequests, distinguishing between disconnected and connected speech.

Core Idea
General bequest includes parts; specific bequest of part creates conflict resolved by sharing or priority.

This lesson uses a concrete fiqh example to illustrate how the Hanafi usul principle of 'amm (general) and khass (specific) equality works in practice. By analyzing a bequest of a ring and its gem, you will understand how the structure of speech (connected or disconnected) changes the outcome. We will also compare it with the bequest of a slave and its service to highlight the limits of inclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bequests
Recognise that the first bequest of a 'ring' is a general term (amm) that includes both the band and the gem. The second bequest of the 'gem' is specific (khass).
The gem is a part of the ring by default.
2
Classify the speech structure
Determine whether the two bequests are made with disconnected speech (kalam mafsul) – separate sentences – or connected speech (kalam mawsul) – within one sentence.
The source uses 'then' to indicate disconnection.
3
Apply the rule for disconnected speech
If the speech is disconnected, the general bequest already covers the gem, so the specific second bequest creates a conflict. The solution is sharing the gem equally between the two legatees.
4
Apply the rule for connected speech
If the speech is connected, the second bequest is considered an exception to the first, so the gem goes entirely to the second person.
5
Compare with slave and service example
Note that a bequest of a slave does not include its service because they are different categories, unlike the ring and gem which form a composite entity. This reinforces that inclusion depends on the nature of the whole.
Service is not part of the slave's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : واذا اوصى بخاتم لانسان ثم بالفص منه لاخران الحلقة للأول والفص بينهما . پہلے یہ بیان کیا کہ عام خاص کے مساوی ہوتا ہے اب اس کی تائید ایک فقہی مسئلہ سے کرتے ہیں وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے وصیت کی خائم کی ایک شخص کیلئے پھر کلام مفصول کیساتھ نگینہ کی وصیت کی دوسرے کیلئے تو حلقہ پہلے کا ہوگا اور نگینہ دونوں کے درمیان مشترک ہوگا اسلئے کہ حلقہ خاتم عام کی طرح ہے اور فص ( نگینہ) خاص کی طرح ہے تو چونکہ فص میں تعارض آگیا لہذا وہ دونوں کے درمیان مشترک ہو گا لیکن اگر کلام موصول کیسا تھ وصیت کی تو پھر فص دوسرے کو ہی ملے گا جیسا کہ اگر نفس رقبہ ( خدمت کے غلام) کی وصیت ایک کیلئے کرے اور رقبہ کے منافع یعنی خدمت کی وصیت دوسرے کیلئے کرے تو خدمت دوسرے ہی کیلئے ہوتی ہے۔ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ وصیت رقبہ خدمت کو شامل نہیں ہوتی اس لئے کہ رقبہ اور خدمت دو مختلف جنس ہیں بخلاف خاتم کے کہ وہ فص کو بھی شامل ہوتا ہے۔
His statement: 'And if one bequeaths a ring to a person, then the gem from it to another, the band is for the first, and the gem is between them.' Previously it was stated that 'amm and khass are equal. Now this is supported by a fiqh issue: If someone makes a bequest of a ring to one person, then with disconnected speech (kalam mafsul) makes a bequest of the gem to another, then the band is for the first and the gem is shared between them. Because the band is like 'amm (general) and the gem is like khass (specific). Since there is conflict regarding the gem, it becomes shared. But if the bequest was with connected speech (kalam mawsul), then the gem goes only to the second. Similarly, if one bequeaths the slave itself (nafs al-raqaba) to one and its usufruct (service) to another, the service goes to the second alone. We say that the bequest of the slave does not include the service because the slave and service are two different categories, unlike the ring which includes the gem.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage uses a fiqh example to demonstrate the principle that 'amm (general) and khass (specific) can be equal in some contexts. The example: A bequest of a ring (general) to one person, and then a separate bequest of its gem (specific) to another. If the two bequests are made with disconnected sentences (kalam mafsul), the ring goes to the first person, but the gem is disputed; since the gem is a part of the ring, the first person may claim it, but the specific bequest to the second creates conflict. The solution is that the gem is shared between them. However, if the bequests were in a connected speech (kalam mawsul), the second bequest takes priority and the gem goes solely to the second. The reason is that in disconnected speech, the general (ring) includes the specific (gem) by default, so the first bequest already covers the gem; the second bequest creates competition. In connected speech, the second bequest is seen as an exception. The parallel example of bequeathing a slave and its service further illustrates: the service is not included in the bequest of the slave because they are different genres, whereas the gem is part of the ring. This shows the nuance in Hanafi fiqh regarding the scope of general terms in bequests.

Key Terms
خاتم (khātam): A ring, including both the band and the gem
فص (fass): The gem or stone set in the ring

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It goes entirely to the first person
B) It goes entirely to the second person
C) It is shared equally between them
D) It goes to the first person's heirs

Key Takeaway
The allocation of a part bequeathed separately from the whole depends on whether the speech is connected or disconnected.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
6 min
Bequest of Ring and Gem: 'Amm and Khass in Conflict
Usul rule illustrated through a bequest example of ring and gem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that general and specific are equal in legal effect to a bequest scenario and distinguish between connected and separate statements.

Core Idea
When a specific bequest conflicts with a general bequest, they share the specific item unless the bequest was made in one connected statement.

This lesson uses a concrete fiqh example to illustrate a key usul principle: general ('amm) and specific (khass) have equal legal authority. The example involves a person who bequeaths a ring (general) to one person and then separately bequeaths its gem (specific) to another. The Hanafi ruling shows how the general and specific interact, and how the manner of statement (separate vs connected) changes the outcome. Understanding this helps you see how usul principles operate in real fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify General and Specific
In the bequest, the ring (khatam) is the general term that includes the gem (fass). The gem is the specific part. Recognize that the general term inherently covers the specific item.
In Hanafi usul, 'amm includes all its individuals unless a specific exclusion (takhsis) is proven.
2
Analyze the Two Scenarios
First scenario: bequests made with separate statements (ring to A, gem to B in separate sentences). Second scenario: connected statement (e.g., 'I give the ring to A and its gem to B'). Note the different outcomes: in separate, gem is shared; in connected, gem goes to B alone.
The key is whether the specific is mentioned in a way that separates it from the general (separate) or explicitly assigns it (connected).
3
Apply the Slave Analogy
The text compares to bequest of a slave (raqaba) vs. his service (manafi‘). The slave and service are considered different categories, so bequest of slave does not include service. This contrasts with ring and gem, which are not different categories.
This analogy helps clarify that the general-specific relationship depends on the nature of the items.
4
Derive the Usul Principle
The principle: 'Amm and khass are equal in legal effect. When a specific item is part of a general bequest but later specifically bequeathed to another, the specific is shared between the two beneficiaries unless the bequests are connected (i.e., the specific is explicitly separated in the same breath).
This illustrates the Hanafi rule that a specific text (khass) can limit a general text ('amm), but when both are present, they conflict and are resolved by sharing.

قوله: واذا اوصى بخاتم لانسان ثم بالفص منه لاخران الحلقة للأول والفص بينهما. پہلے یہ بیان کیا کہ عام خاص کے مساوی ہوتا ہے اب اس کی تائید ایک فقہی مسئلہ سے کرتے ہیں وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے وصیت کی خاتم کی ایک شخص کے لیے پھر کلام مفصول کے ساتھ نگینہ کی وصیت کی دوسرے کے لیے تو حلقہ پہلے کا ہوگا اور نگینہ دونوں کے درمیان مشترک ہوگا اس لیے کہ حلقہ خاتم عام کی طرح ہے اور فص (نگینہ) خاص کی طرح ہے تو چونکہ فص میں تعارض آگیا لہذا وہ دونوں کے درمیان مشترک ہوگا لیکن اگر کلام موصول کے ساتھ وصیت کی تو پھر فص دوسرے کو ہی ملے گا جیسا کہ اگر نفس رقبہ (خدمت کے غلام) کی وصیت ایک کے لیے کرے اور رقبہ کے منافع یعنی خدمت کی وصیت دوسرے کے لیے کرے تو خدمت دوسرے ہی کے لیے ہوتی ہے۔ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ وصیت رقبہ خدمت کو شامل نہیں ہوتی اس لیے کہ رقبہ اور خدمت دو مختلف جنس ہیں بخلاف خاتم کے کہ وہ فص کو بھی شامل ہوتا ہے۔
He said: And if someone bequeaths a ring to a person, then subsequently bequeaths the gem from it to another person, the ring goes to the first and the gem is shared between them. Previously it was stated that general and specific are equal. Now this is supported by a fiqh issue: if someone bequeaths a ring (khatam) to one person, then with a separate statement bequeaths the gem (fass) to another, the ring goes to the first and the gem is shared between them because the ring is like the general and the gem is like the specific. Since there is conflict regarding the gem, it becomes shared between them. But if the bequest is made with a connected statement, then the gem goes to the second alone, just as if one bequeaths the slave himself (raqaba) to one person and the service (manafi') of the slave to another, the service belongs to the second. We say that the bequest of the slave does not include the service because the slave and his service are two different categories, unlike the ring which includes the gem.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a legal example to reinforce the usul principle that general (‘amm) and specific (khass) have equal legal effect when they conflict. The example is about bequest (wasiyya). If a person bequeaths a ring (general) to A, and then separately bequeaths its gem (specific) to B, the ring goes to A and the gem is shared between A and B because the specific item (gem) is part of the general item (ring) and both claims conflict. However, if the bequests are made in a connected manner (e.g., 'I give the ring to A and its gem to B'), then the gem goes exclusively to B because the specific was explicitly separated. The passage draws an analogy with bequest of a slave (raqaba) versus his service (manafi‘), noting that they are distinct categories, unlike ring and gem.

Key Terms
عام (‘amm): General term that includes all individuals of a category without limit.
خاص (khass): Specific term that refers to a particular subset or individual.
وصیت (wasiyya): A bequest made by a person for after their death, up to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the bequest example, if the gem (fass) is bequeathed with a separate statement after the ring, who gets the gem?
A) Only the first person (the one who got the ring).
B) Only the second person (the one named for the gem).
C) Both persons share the gem equally.
D) Neither; the bequest is invalid.

Key Takeaway
When a general bequest and a specific bequest conflict, they are equal, leading to sharing unless the specific is explicitly separated in a connected statement.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Hanafi Response to Shafi'i Objections on Takhsis
Two objections by Imam Shafi'i and Hanafi responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the specification of the Quranic verses 'Do not eat...' and 'Whoever enters...' and evaluate Imam Shafi'i's objections by applying the usul principle that the general is qat'i until a clear specifier arises.

Core Idea
A general Quranic verse cannot be specified by qiyas or khabar wahid; only decisive evidence suffices.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the specification (takhsis) of two Quranic verses. Imam Shafi'i argued that the Hanafis had arbitrarily excluded certain individuals from the general ruling, and that if they could do so, he could analogically exclude others. The Hanafi response, grounded in the principle that the general text is definitive (qat'i), demonstrates that no real specification occurred: the forgetful person was never included in the general prohibition, and the safety verse pertains only to life, not limbs. This debate highlights the Hanafi commitment to a rigorous proof hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Quranic verses in dispute
Locate the two verses: 'Do not eat of that upon which Allah's name has not been mentioned' (al-An'am 6:121) and 'Whoever enters it shall be safe' (Aal Imran 3:97). Note that both contain general words: 'ma' and 'man'.
These verses are used as case studies for the broader principle of takhsis.
2
State Imam Shafi'i's first objection and proposed specification
Imam Shafi'i argues that the Hanafis have excluded the forgetful (nasi) from the first verse without a valid specifier. He claims the general word 'ma' includes both the forgetful and the intentional, so the Hanafi ruling is a specification. He then asserts he can also exclude the one who forgets after mentioning by qiyas and a hadith.
The hadith cited: 'The Muslim slaughters in the name of Allah, whether he mentioned (the name) or not.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first objection
The Hanafi response is that the forgetful person is not part of the general intended meaning because he is legally equivalent to the one who mentions (dhakir). The hadith shows that the Muslim's act is intrinsically in Allah's name, so the forgetful is not included in the verse's prohibition. Thus, no specification occurred; the verse only applies to those who intentionally omit the name.
This is not an arbitrary exclusion but a recognition that the general term does not encompass the forgetful analogy.
4
Analyze Imam Shafi'i's second objection and the Hanafi response
For the second verse, Shafi'i objects that the Hanafis have excluded two categories: the one who kills inside the Haram and the mutilator of limbs. He wants to also exclude the one who murders outside and then enters. The Hanafi response: the safety (amn) in the verse refers only to safety of life (dhat), not to limbs or property. Therefore, the mutilator is not covered, and no one has been excluded from the general. The murderer inside is covered but is subject to qisas because the safety does not protect against just punishment.
This clarifies that the Hanafi position did not require takhsis; the general term is interpreted in light of its intended scope.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf and the principle established
The practical consequence: Hanafis consider meat from an animal slaughtered by a Muslim who forgot Bismillah as halal; Shafi'is consider it haram because the verse is general. The underlying usul principle: the Hanafi school holds that a general verse is qat'i and cannot be specified by qiyas or khabar wahid, as they lack the authority to override a definitive text. Only a clear specifier (mukhassis) from the Quran, mutawatir sunnah, or consensus can restrict its application.
This dispute exemplifies the Hanafi insistence on a hierarchical proof structure where secondary sources cannot challenge primary texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یہاں پر امام شافعی کے دو اشکال ہیں۔ (۱) پہلا اشکال: قرآن کریم میں ہے (وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا مِمَّا لَمْ يُذْكَرِ اسْمُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ) "جس جانور پر اللہ کا نام ذبح کرتے وقت نہ لیا جائے اسے نہ کھاؤ۔" امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ (ما) لفظ عام ہے، ناسی اور عامد دونوں کو شامل ہے، لیکن احناف نے اس میں سے ناسی کو خاص کر دیا اور کہا کہ بھول کر تسمیہ چھوٹ جائے تو جانور حلال ہے۔ تو جب تم ناسی کو خاص کرتے ہو تو ہم بھی خاص کرتے ہیں، ایک تو ناسی پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اور دوسرے اس حدیث سے استدلال کرتے ہوئے کہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا: (المسلم يذبح على اسم الله، سمى أو لم يسم) "مسلمان اللہ کے نام پر ذبح کرتا ہے، بسم اللہ پڑھے یا نہ پڑھے۔" (۲) دوسرا اشکال: قرآن کریم میں ہے (وَمَن دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا) یعنی جو بیت اللہ میں داخل ہوا وہ امان ہے۔ اس میں (من) کا لفظ عام ہے، یہ سب کو شامل ہے، لیکن تم نے اس میں سے ایک تو اس شخص کو خاص کر لیا جس نے حرم میں داخل ہونے کے بعد قتل کیا، اور دوسرا اس شخص کو خاص کر دیا جو کسی کے اعضاء کاٹنے کے بعد داخل ہو، اور تم نے کہا کہ ان دونوں کو حرم میں داخل ہونے کے باوجود امن حاصل نہیں ہوگا بلکہ ان سے قصاص لیا جائے گا۔ تو ہم اس میں سے ایک تیسرے شخص کو بھی خاص کرتے ہیں اور یہ وہ شخص ہے جو قتل کے بعد حرم میں داخل ہو، ایک تو ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے، اور دوسرا اس حدیث سے استدلال کرتے ہوئے: الحرم لا يعيذ عاصياً ولا فاراً بدم۔
قوله: ولا يجوز تخصيص قوله تعالى وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا مِمَّا لَمْ يُذْكَرِ اسْمُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ وَمَن دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا بالقياس وخبر الواحد، لأنه ليسا بمخصصين۔ احناف کی طرف سے پہلے اشکال کا جواب: مصنف نے اس کا جواب یہ دیا ہے کہ آپ کا یہ کہنا صحیح نہیں ہے کہ یہ دونوں آپس میں مخصوص ہیں، اور ہم نے ان میں سے کسی فرد کو خاص نہیں کیا ہے، اس لیے کہ ناسی تو ذاکر کے حکم میں ہوتا ہے، لہذا وہ اس آیت میں داخل ہی نہیں۔ وَمَن دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا۔ احناف کی طرف دوسرے اشکال کا جواب: اسی طرح (وَمَن دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا) آیت میں امن سے مراد امن الذات ہے، اور اعضاء ذات میں سے نہیں ہے بلکہ مال میں
Here are two objections of Imam Shafi'i. (1) First objection: The Quran says (And do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned). Imam Shafi'i says that the word 'ma' is general, including both the forgetful (nasi) and the intentional (amid). But the Hanafis have taken the forgetful out of it, saying that if the name is forgotten, the animal is halal. So when you exclude the forgetful, we also exclude a third person (the one who forgets after mentioning?), relying on qiyas on the forgetful and the hadith 'The Muslim slaughters in the name of Allah, whether he mentioned (the name) or not.' (2) Second objection: The Quran says (And whoever enters it shall be safe). The word 'man' is general, including all. But you have excluded two persons: one who kills after entering the Haram, and one who mutilates limbs and then enters, saying they are not safe but subject to qisas. So we also exclude a third person: one who enters after committing murder, relying on qiyas on those two and the hadith 'The Haram does not protect a sinner nor a fugitive from blood-vengeance.'
He said: It is not permissible to specify the words of Allah 'And do not eat...' and 'And whoever enters it shall be safe' by qiyas and khabar wahid, because they are not specifiers. The Hanafi response to the first objection: The author (al-Marghinani) answers that your statement that these two are specified is not correct, and we have not taken out any individual because the forgetful is considered like the one who mentions (the name), so he is not included in the verse. Response to the second objection: Similarly, in the verse (And whoever enters it shall be safe), safety (amn) refers to safety of life (dhat), and limbs are not included in life but rather in property.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute regarding the specification (takhsis) of two Quranic verses: (1) 'Do not eat of that upon which Allah's name has not been mentioned' and (2) 'Whoever enters the Haram shall be safe.' Imam Shafi'i argues that these verses are general and that the Hanafis have unlawfully specified them by excluding the forgetful (nasi) and the murderer/mutilator. He claims that if the Hanafis can exclude some, he can also exclude others by analogy. The Hanafi response, attributed to the author (likely al-Marghinani of al-Hidayah or the commentator of Noor al-Anwar), argues that no specification has actually occurred. For the first verse, the forgetful person is not really included because he is considered like someone who did mention Allah's name, based on the hadith that the Muslim always slaughters in Allah's name. Thus, the verse only addresses those who intentionally omit the name. For the second verse, the safety promised is specifically safety of life (amn al-dhat), not safety of limbs or property, so the mutilator of limbs is not covered by the verse. The passage also states that qiyas and khabar wahid cannot be used to specify these verses because they do not have the status of specifiers (mukhassis). This argument underscores the Hanafi principle that the general (amm) remains decisive unless there is a clear specifier from the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus.

Key Terms
عام (‘amm): A word that includes all members of a category without restriction.
تخصیص (takhsis): The act of specifying or restricting a general term to a particular subset.
ناسی (nasi): One who forgets to mention Allah's name while slaughtering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to Imam Shafi'i's first objection, why is the forgetful person not included in the verse 'Do not eat of that upon which Allah's name has not been mentioned'?
A) Because the verse was abrogated by another verse.
B) Because the forgetful person is legally equivalent to the one who mentions Allah's name, based on a hadith.
C) Because the word 'ma' in the verse is actually specific, not general.
D) Because the Hanafis used qiyas to exclude the forgetful.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a general Quranic verse remains binding unless a clear specifier (mukhassis) from the Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma is provided; qiyas and khabar wahid cannot specify a definitive general text.

Hanafi Responses to Objections on Takhsis of Tasmiyah and Sanctuary Verses
Hanafi defense of their specification of general verses using qiyas and hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi response to objections regarding specification of two Quranic verses (tasmiyah and sanctuary) and identify the usul principles (qiyas, hadith, definition of security) used in the defense.

Core Idea
Hanafis specify general Quranic verses by qiyas and hadith, not by arbitrary exclusion.

This lesson examines the Hanafi defense against the criticism that they improperly specified two general Quranic verses. The first objection concerns the verse prohibiting consumption of meat without tasmiyah, where opponents argue that forgetting should still be included. The second objection involves the verse promising security for anyone entering the Haram, which opponents claim the Hanafis have unjustly limited. Through careful usul analysis, the Hanafis demonstrate that their specifications are grounded in sound analogy and prophetic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested verses and objections
Locate the two Quranic verses and the objections raised that the Hanafis have improperly specified them.
Verse 1: 6:121; Verse 2: 3:97
2
Analyze the first Hanafi response (tasmiyah verse)
Understand that the nasi (forgetful) is considered like the dhakir (remembering) by qiyas and hadith, thus not covered by the prohibition.
Hadith: 'al-muslim yudhabbiḥu 'ala ism Allāh sammā aw lam yusammi'
3
Analyze the second Hanafi response (sanctuary verse)
Understand that 'security' is limited to life (dhāt), not limbs or property, and the verse applies only to those mubāh al-dam upon entry.
Hadith: 'al-haram la yu'idhu 'asiyan wa la fārran bi dam'
4
Evaluate the usul principles used
Note that Hanafis use qiyas jali and khabar wahid to specify general verses, while opponents reject this.
Opponents argue that qiyas and ahad hadith cannot specify the Quran.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis permit meat from a nasi's slaughter; opponents require tasmiyah. For sanctuary, Hanafis exclude murderers and limb-cutters from security; opponents may include them.
The difference affects daily fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کہا کہ بھول کر تسمیہ چھوٹ جائے تو جانور حلال ہے تو جب تم ناسی کو [؟] ہو بھی خاص کر لیتے ہیں ایک تو ناسی پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اور دوسرے اس حدیث سے استدلال کرتے ہوئے کہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ: (المسلم يذبخ على اسم الله سمى أولم یسم): "مسلمان اللہ کے نام پر ذبح کرتا ہے بسم اللہ پڑھے یا نہ پڑھے) (۲) دوسرا اشکال ... قرآن کریم میں ہے ( و من دخله كان آمنا ) یعنی جو بیت اللہ میں داخل ہوا وہ امان ہے اس میں (من) کا لفظ یہ عام ہے یہ سب کو شامل ہے لیکن تم نے اس میں سے ایک تو اس شخص کو خاص کر لیا جس نے حرم میں داخل ہونے کے بعد قتل کیا اور دوسرا اس شخص کو خاص کر دیا جو کسی کے اعضاء کاٹنے کے بعد داخل ہو اور تم نے کہا کہ ان دونوں کو حرم میں داخل ہونے کے باوجود امن حاصل نہیں ہوگا بلکہ ان سے قصاص لیا جائے گا تو ہم اس میں سے ایک تیسرے شخص کو بھی خاص کرتے ہیں اور یہ وہ شخص ہے جو قتل کے بعد حرم میں داخل ہو ایک تو ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اور دوسرا اس حدیث سے استدلال کرتے ہوئے الحرم لا يعيذ عاصياً ولا فاراً بدم . قوله : ولا يجوز تخصيص قوله تعالى ولاتأكلو امما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه ومن دخله كان امنا بالقياس وخبر الواحد لانها ليسا بمخصوصين احناف کی طرف سے پہلے اشکال کا جواب مصنف نے اس کا جواب یہ دیا ہے کہ آپ کا یہ کہنا صحیح نہیں ہے کہ یہ دونوں آپس میں مخصوص ہیں اور ہم نے ان میں سے کسی فرد کو خاص نہیں کیا ہے اس لئے کہ ناسی تو ذاکر کے حکم میں ہوتا ہے لہذاوہ اس آیت میں داخل ہی نہیں۔ ولا تأكلوا مما لم يذكر اسم الله . احناف کی طرف دوسرے اشکال کا جواب ... ای طرح (ومن دخله كان امنا ) آیت میں امن سے مراد من الذات ہے اور اعضاء ذات میں سے نہیں ہے بلکہ مال میں سے نہیں ۔ اسی طرح جو شخص داخل ہونے کے بعد قتل کرے وہ بھی اس [؟] میں داخل کیونکہ آیت کا مفہوم تو یہ ہے کہ جو شخص مباح الدم ہونے کے بعد حرم میں داخل ہو وہ امن میں ہوگا ۔
He said: If the tasmiyah is forgotten unintentionally, then the animal is halal. So when you also specify the one who forgets [nasi]? One, by analogizing upon the nasi, and second, by seeking evidence from this hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "The Muslim slaughters in the name of Allah, whether he says Bismillah or not." Second objection... In the Holy Quran is: "And whoever enters it is secure" meaning whoever enters the House of Allah is in security. The word "whoever" (man) is general, it includes everyone. But you specified from it two: first, the person who killed after entering the Haram, and second, the person who entered after cutting off someone's limbs. And you said that these two will not have security despite entering the Haram, but rather qisas will be taken from them. So we also specify a third person from it, and he is the person who enters the Haram after committing murder, one by analogizing upon each of those two, and second by seeking evidence from this hadith: "The Haram does not protect a sinner nor one fleeing with blood." His statement: And it is not permissible to specify the saying of Allah Most High "And do not eat from that over which the name of Allah was not mentioned" and "And whoever enters it is secure" by analogy and by solitary report, because they are not specified. From the Hanafis' side, the answer to the first objection: The author has answered it by saying that your statement that these two are specified among themselves is not correct, and we have not specified any individual from them because the one who forgets is in the ruling of the one who remembers. Therefore, he is not included in this verse: "And do not eat from that over which the name of Allah was not mentioned." From the Hanafis' side, the answer to the second objection... Similarly, in the verse "And whoever enters it is secure," security is meant for the self (dhāt) and not for the limbs, and not for property. Similarly, the person who kills after entering is also included in this [?] because the meaning of the verse is that whoever enters the Haram while being of permissible blood (mubāh al-dam) will be secure.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents Hanafi responses to two objections raised by opponents (likely Shafi'is) regarding their specification of general Quranic verses. The first objection concerns the verse prohibiting consumption of meat without tasmiyah (bismillah). Opponents argue that the verse is general and includes all cases, but Hanafis specify that the nasi (one who forgets) is not included. Hanafis respond that the nasi is legally considered like the one who remembers (dhakir) by analogy (qiyas) and by the hadith 'The Muslim slaughters in the name of Allah, whether he says Bismillah or not.' Therefore, the nasi is not within the scope of the prohibition. The second objection concerns the verse 'And whoever enters it is secure' (Surah Al-Imran 3:97). Opponents argue that Hanafis have improperly specified this general verse by excluding a murderer and a mutilator from its security. Hanafis respond that the security in the verse refers specifically to protection of one's life (dhāt), not limbs or property. Moreover, the verse applies only to those who enter the Haram while being mubāh al-dam (i.e., not guilty of a capital crime). They also add a third category: a person who commits murder and then enters the Haram is excluded, using qiyas on the first two cases and the hadith 'The Haram does not protect a sinner nor one fleeing with blood.' The author then quotes a statement (likely from the matn) that specification of these verses by qiyas and khabar wahid is not valid because the verses are not specified in themselves, but the Hanafis clarify that they have not actually specified the verses; rather, the nasi and the various categories are not included in the intended meaning. The commentary follows the author's order: first the objections, then the responses, with a focus on defending Hanafi usul methodology.

Key Terms
ناسی (nāsi): One who unintentionally forgets to recite tasmiyah at the time of slaughter.
تسمیہ (tasmiyya): Uttering 'Bismillah' or 'Allahu Akbar' at the time of slaughter.
عام ('āmm): A general term that includes all individuals within its scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why is the nasi (one who forgets) not included in the verse 'wa la ta'kulu mimma lam yudhkar ismullah'?
A) Because he is like a non-Muslim.
B) Because he is considered like the one who remembers by qiyas and hadith.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because the nasi is excluded by ijma.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi takhsis of general Quranic verses is based on qiyas and authentic hadith that define the intended scope, not on arbitrary exclusion.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Status of 'Amm Makhsus: Known and Unknown Specification
Explaining the ruling on general texts when followed by known or unknown specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a specified general text ('āmm makhsūṣ) remains usable as a proof and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on the dual analogy to istithnā' and naskh.

Core Idea
A specified general text loses certainty but retains probative value as a legal proof.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a general text that is later specified presents a delicate balance. The specification removes the text's definitive (qat‘ī) status, yet the text does not become worthless. This intermediate position is derived by comparing takhsīṣ to both exception (istithnā') and abrogation (naskh). This lesson unpacks that reasoning and applies it to known and unknown specification cases.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling
Identify the author's opening statement: after specification, the 'amm is not qat‘ī but can still be used as a proof (hujja).
This applies to both known and unknown specification.
2
Define known and unknown specification
From the Quranic example and hadith, distinguish khusūs ma‘lūm (specification whose scope is known) from khusūs majhūl (scope unknown).
Al-Baqarah 2:275 initially gives unknown specification; the hadith of six commodities makes it known.
3
Analyze the dual analogy
Takhsīṣ resembles istithnā' in ruling (excluded part is not included) and naskh in wording (the specifying text is independent). Work through how each analogy pulls in opposite directions.
Istithnā' likeness would preserve qat‘iyya; naskh likeness would undermine istidlāl.
4
Apply to known specification
When specification is known, the istithnā' resemblance dominates enough to keep the general qat‘ī, but the naskh resemblance prevents full istidlāl. The compromise: not qat‘ī, but still a proof.
Known excepted items preserve the certainty of the remaining general.
5
Apply to unknown specification
When specification is unknown, the istithnā' resemblance weakens istidlāl (ignorance of excepted transfers to general), while the naskh resemblance suggests the abrogator falls away leaving the general intact. Again the outcome: not qat‘ī, but still a proof.
Unknown abrogator has no effect, so general remains usable though not definitive.

If a known or unknown specification follows it, it does not remain definitive, but its use as evidence is not dropped, due to its resemblance to exception and abrogation. After finishing the explanation of the specified, we now explain the specified general according to some. He says: If a general is followed by a known or unknown specification, it does not remain definitive, but it is correct to use it as evidence. In terminology, specification is to restrict a general to some of its individuals by an independent and connected statement. The school of Imam Shafi'i: According to Imam Shafi'i, if the statement is an independent statement of news or a connected statement, then it is specified. Example of both known and unknown specification: Allah's command (And Allah permitted sale and forbade usury). The word 'sale' is general, including every type of sale: valid sale, invalid sale, usury, etc. But usury is specified from it. Usury is excess and increase without compensation. We do not know which type of increase is forbidden, so this is unknown specification. Then in the hadith, the six items are mentioned, i.e., wheat for wheat, barley for barley... etc. So this becomes an example of known specification, because due to the prohibition of usury in these items, we know the cause of the prohibition of usury, which according to Hanafis is quantity with same genus, and according to Shafi'is is foodstuff and storage. The proof: Specification resembles both exception and abrogation. In terms of ruling, it resembles exception: just as the excepted is not included in the excepted-from, so the specified is not included under the general. In terms of wording, it resembles the abrogator: just as the wording of the abrogator is independent, so its wording is also independent. The first school (Hanafi): We have considered both resemblances. Thus we say: If the specification is known, the resemblance to exception requires that the general remains definitive, because when the excepted is known, the excepted-from remains as it is in the remaining individuals. The resemblance to abrogation requires that using the general as evidence is not completely correct, because the abrogator is independent, and every independent accepts (?) reasoning, because it is an effect with its cause. So it will not work: how many individuals left due to reasoning and how many remain? Then the specification becomes unknown, and its ignorance affects the ignorance of the general. If the specification is unknown, the opposite applies: the resemblance to exception requires that using the general as evidence is not correct, because the ignorance of the excepted affects the ignorance of the excepted-from, and nothing beneficial is obtained from the unknown. The resemblance to abrogation requires that the general remains definitive, because the unknown abrogator itself falls away, and what falls away by itself has no effect on the remaining speech. In short, considering both resemblances, we said: the general does not remain definitive, but it is correct to use it as evidence. He said: So it becomes like when one sells two slaves for a thousand, stipulating the option in one of them specifically and naming its price. The author compared the madhhab to a fiqhi issue, which is: a person buys two slaves for a thousand, and stipulates the option in one specific slave, and also mentions its price separately. Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul ruling on the evidentiary value of a general text ('amm) that has been specified (makhsus). The author opens by stating the core rule: when a general text is followed by a specification—whether the specification is known (ma'lum) or unknown (majhul)—the general text does not retain its definitive (qat'i) status. However, it can still be used as a proof (hujja) in legal reasoning, due to its resemblance to both exception (istithna') and abrogation (naskh). The author then clarifies the definition of takhsis as restricting a general term to some of its individuals by an independent and connected statement. He notes that Imam Shafi'i held that such specification does not affect the general's use as evidence if the specifying statement is independent or connected. The Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 275) is presented as an example: the word 'bay' (sale) is general, but usury (riba) is specified from it. At first, the exact scope of prohibited riba is unknown (majhul), but the hadith of the six commodities (ashya' sitta) provides known (ma'lum) examples. This illustrates how specification can be either. The Hanafi position is then defended through a tashbih (analogy) analysis: takhsis resembles istithna' in ruling (the excluded is not part of the general) and naskh in wording (the specifying text is independent). The Hanafis balance these two similarities: if the specification is known, the exception similarity suggests the general remains qat'i, but the abrogation similarity suggests istidlal is imperfect; if the specification is unknown, the exception similarity weakens istidlal while the abrogation similarity suggests the general remains qat'i. The final compromise is that the general is not qat'i but still usable as proof. The passage concludes by likening this nuanced status to a fiqhi case of selling two slaves with an option (khiyar) in one, where the legal implications depend on whether the option is specified or unspecified—mirroring the usul principle.

Key Terms
خصوص معلوم (Khusūs Ma‘lūm): A specification that is known in its precise scope, either by explicit textual evidence or by identifying the exact individuals excluded from the general.
خصوص مجهول (Khusūs Majhūl): A specification whose precise scope is unknown, such as an ambiguous term or unclear exclusion, leading to doubt about the general's application.
عام مخصوص (‘Āmm Makhsūṣ): A general term that has been restricted by a specifying text, so that some of its originally included individuals are excluded.
تخصیص (Takhṣīṣ): Restricting a general statement to some of its individuals by an independent and connected (mawsul) statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The general text loses all evidentiary value and cannot be used as proof.
C) The general text is no longer qat'i but can still be used as a valid proof (hujja).
D) The general text remains qat'i only if the specification is from the Quran, otherwise it becomes non-proof.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a specified general text is not definitive but remains a viable legal proof, balancing resemblance to both exception and abrogation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Effect of Specification on General Statements: The First School's View
Explaining the effect of specification on 'āmm based on its resemblance to exception and abrogation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that specification (takhsīs) does not render a general ('āmm) statement completely invalid for deduction, and distinguish between cases where the specification evidence is known versus unknown.

Core Idea
Specification does not abrogate the general; it only removes some instances, leaving the remaining general valid for deduction but not qaṭ'ī.

In Usul al-Fiqh, a major debate concerns how specification (takhsīs) affects a general ('āmm) statement. Does it render the general uncertain like abrogation, or does it merely exclude some individuals without affecting the rest? This passage presents the reasoning of the first school, which likens specification to both exception (istithnā') and abrogation (naskh), deriving a nuanced ruling: the general does not remain qaṭ'ī (definitive), but it is still valid for legal deduction (istidlāl). Let's walk through their argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two similarities
The school considers specification similar to exception (mustathnā) and to abrogation (nāsikh) in terms of wording independence. Understand why these two analogies are relevant.
Exception removes items without affecting the remaining text; abrogation cancels the text entirely.
2
Analyze the case when specification evidence is known
When the evidence of specification is known, the similarity to exception requires the general to remain qaṭ'ī; the similarity to abrogation would make deduction invalid. The school reconciles: general is not qaṭ'ī but deduction is valid.
3
Analyze the case when specification evidence is unknown
The reverse applies: similarity to exception makes deduction invalid; similarity to abrogation makes the general qaṭ'ī. An unknown abrogator drops away without affecting the rest.
4
Derive the final rule
The school concludes that in both scenarios, the general does not remain qaṭ'ī, but deduction from it is still correct. This distinguishes specification from abrogation.
The general retains usability but loses full certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستثنی مستثنی منہ میں داخل نہیں ہوتا اس طرح مخصوص بھی عام کے تحت داخل نہیں ہوتا اور یہ اپنے صیغہ کے اعتبار سے ناسخ کے ساتھ مشابہ ہے کہ جیسے ناسخ کا صیغہ مستقل ہوتا ہے اس طرح اس کا صیغہ بھی مستقل ہوتا ہے۔
پہلا مذہب تو ہم نے ان دونوں مشابہتوں کا لحاظ کیا چنانچہ ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ اگر دلیل خصوص معلوم ہوتو مستثنی کی مشابہت تقاضا کرتی ہے کہ عام قطعی رہے اس لئے مستقلی جب معلوم ہو تو باقی افراد میں مستثنی علی حالہ باقی رہتا ہے اور تاریخ کی مشابہت تقاضا کرتی ہے کہ عام سے استدلال کرنا بالکل صحیح نہ ہو اس لئے کہ نائخ مستقل ہوتا ہے اور ہر مستقل تعلیل کو قبول کرتا ہے کیونکہ وہ معلول بعلتہ (!) ہوتا ہے تو پر نہیں چلے گا کہ تعلیل کی وجہ سے اس سے کتنے افراد نکل گئے اور کتنے باقی ہیں تو وہاں دلیل خصوص مجہول ہو جائیگی اور اس کی جہالت عام کی جہالت میں مؤثر ہو گی اور اگر دلیل خصوص مجہول ہو تو معاملہ بالعکس ہو جائیگا یعنی کی مشابہت تقاضا کرے گی عام سے استدلال کرنا صحیح نہ ہو کیونکہ مستثنیٰ کی جہالت مستثنی منہ کی جہالت میں تاثیر کرتی ہے اور مجہول سے کوئی فائکہ د حاصل نہیں ہوتا اور تاریخ کی مشابہت تقاضا کرے گی کہ عام قطعی رہے کیونکہ ناسخ مجہول خود ساقط ہو جاتا ہے اور جو بنفسہ ساقط ہو جائے اس کا باقی کلام پر کوئی اثر نہیں ہوتا الغرض ہم نے دونوں مشابہتوں کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ عام قطعی تو نہیں رہے گا لیکن اس سے استدلال کرنا صحیح ہو گا۔
The excepted item is not included in the thing excepted from; similarly, the specified (makhsūs) is not included under the general. And in terms of its wording, it resembles the abrogator (nāsikh), because just as the wording of the abrogator is independent, so is its wording independent. The first school: We considered both similarities. Thus, we say that if the evidence of specification is known, the similarity to exception requires that the general remain qat'ī (definitive), because when the independent [abrogator] is known, the excepted items remain as they are among the remaining individuals. And the similarity to abrogation requires that deduction from the general be absolutely not correct, because the abrogator is independent, and every independent accepts ta'līl (causation), since it is a cause-effect relationship. So it will not work that due to ta'līl, how many individuals came out and how many remain; then the evidence of specification will become unknown, and its ignorance will affect the ignorance of the general. And if the evidence of specification is unknown, the case reverses: the similarity [to exception] will require that deduction from the general be not correct, because the ignorance of the excepted item affects the ignorance of the thing excepted from, and nothing useful is obtained from the unknown. And the similarity to abrogation will require that the general remain qat'ī, because the abrogator, when unknown, itself drops, and what drops by itself has no effect on the remaining speech. In short, we said, taking both similarities into account, that the general will not remain qat'ī, but deduction from it will be correct.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the first school (mazhab) regarding the effect of specification (takhsīs) on general ('āmm) statements in Usul al-Fiqh. The author explains that the specified item is like the excepted item (mustathnā) in that it is not included in the general, but it also resembles the abrogator (nāsikh) in the independence of its wording. The school considered two similarities: similarity to exception and similarity to abrogation. When the evidence of specification is known, the similarity to exception requires that the general remain qat'ī (definitive) for the remaining individuals, because the excepted item is known and remains separate. The similarity to abrogation would require that deduction from the general be invalid, because the abrogator is independent and its effect can be analyzed causally, leading to uncertainty about how many individuals are removed. When the specification evidence is unknown, the opposite applies: the similarity to exception makes deduction invalid due to ignorance of the excepted item, while the similarity to abrogation makes the general qat'ī because an unknown abrogator drops away without affecting the rest. The school reconciles these by stating that the general does not remain qat'ī (definitive), but it is still valid to use it for deduction (istidlāl). This distinguishes between the two concepts: specification does not completely abrogate the general, but it introduces some uncertainty.

Key Terms
عام ('āmm): A term that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
مستثنیٰ (mustathnā): The item excluded from a general statement by way of exception.
ناسخ (nāsikh): A later revelation that abrogates a previous ruling; its wording is independent of the abrogated text.
تخصیص (takhsīs): Specification; the act of removing some individuals from the scope of a general term by evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first school in this passage, what is the status of a general ('āmm) statement when the evidence of specification is known?
A) The general becomes completely invalid for deduction.
B) The general remains qaṭ'ī and deduction from it is valid.
C) The general is not qaṭ'ī but deduction from it is still valid.
D) The general becomes mutashābih.

Key Takeaway
Specification (takhsīs) does not abrogate a general text; the general remains valid for deduction, though it loses its definitive certainty.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Applying Naskh and Istithna to Sale with Khiyar
Usul al-Fiqh: applying naskh and istithna analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the Hanafi ruling on sale with option in one of two slaves by applying the usul principles of naskh and istithna.

Core Idea
Sale with option valid only when both object and price are specified; knowledge distinguishes naskh from istithna.

In usul al-fiqh, analogies to naskh (abrogation) and istithna (exception) help determine the validity of conditional contracts. This passage presents a classic Hanafi example: a sale of two slaves where the buyer has an option in one. The ruling hinges on whether the option is known or unknown, reflecting a deeper usul principle that even a single condition can produce opposite rulings depending on certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Fiqhi Scenario and Its Four Forms
Read the example: selling two slaves for 1000, with option (khiyar) in one. Note the four possible combinations of specifying the object (slave) and its price. Write them down.
Forms: (1) both specified, (2) neither specified, (3) object specified but price not, (4) price specified but object not.
2
Recognize the Two Analogies: Naskh and Istithna
The option can be seen as part of the contract (like an abrogating condition) or outside the ruling (like an exception). Understand the analogy: if the option is considered part of the contract, revoking it resembles naskh; if not part of the ruling, revoking it resembles istithna.
Naskh analogy favors validity; istithna analogy favors invalidity.
3
Analyze the Condition for Each Analogy
Determine when each analogy applies. The key is knowledge: when both the object of option and its price are specified (form 1), the condition is known and independent, resembling naskh. When either is unknown, the condition is ambiguous and dependent, resembling istithna.
The Hanafi reconciliation considers both analogies and applies the one that matches the level of certainty.
4
Derive the Hanafi Ruling Through Reconciliation (Tatbiq)
Apply the principle: if the option is like naskh, the sale is valid because the remaining contract can stand; if like istithna, the sale is invalid because the exception vitiates the whole. The Hanafis rule that in form 1 the naskh analogy prevails; in forms 2-4 the istithna analogy prevails.
Reason: an unknown abrogator drops entirely, leading to an outcome contrary to the parties' intent.
5
Evaluate the Outcome and Identify the Basis
Conclude: only form 1 is valid. The basis is the usul principle that uncertainty (jahalah) in a condition corrupts the contract. This shows how usul principles directly produce specific fiqhi rulings.
Contrast with the hypothetical that all forms should be valid or all invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فصار كما اذا باع عبدين بألف على أنه بالخيار في أحدهما بعينه وسمى ثمنه مصافی نے مذیب مینار کو ایک فقہی مسئلے سے تشبیہ دی ہے اور وہ مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ کوئی شخص دو غلاموں کو ہزار روپیہ پر نیچے اور ان میں سے ایک معین، غلام کے اندر خیار کو نا بہت کرے اور اس کا علیحدہ سے شمن بھی ذکر کردے دراصل اس مسئلے کی چار صورتیں ہیں۔
(۱) پہلی صورت یہ ہے کہ محل خیار دونوں غلاموں میں سے کوئی ایک غلام بھی متعین ہو اور اس کا شمن بھی متعین ہو۔
(۲) دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ دونوں متعین نہ ہوں ۔
(۳) تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ محل خیار متعین ہو لیکن اس کا شمن ذکر نہ کرے۔ (۴) چوتھی صورت یہ ہے کہ اس غلام کا شمن تو ذکر کرے لیکن محل خیار متعین نہ ہو جس نظام میں خیار ہے وہ عقد میں داخل ہے اور ثمن حکم بیع میں داخل نہیں ہے تو اس اعتبار سے کہہ و عقد میں داخل ہے بیچ کو خیار شرط کی وجہ سے واپس کرنا گویا عقد بیع کو تبدیل کرنا ہو گا یہ نسخ کے ساتھ مشابہت ہوگئی ۔ اور اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ حکم میں داخل نہیں ہے اس کو واپس کرنا گویا بیان کرنا ہوگا کہ وہ حکم میں داخل ہی نہ تھا تو اس طرح مستثنیٰ کے ساتھ مشابہت ہوگی۔ تو شیخ کی مشابہت کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ چاروں صورتوں میں بیچ صحیح ہو جائے کیونکہ یہ دونوں غلام بیع کامل بن سکتے ہیں اس پر بیع بالحصہ کے عدم جواز والا اعتراض نہ کیا جائے کیونکہ بیچ بالحصہ ابتداء جائز نہیں اور یہ بیچ بالحصہ ابتداء نہیں ہے بلکہ بقاء ہے اور یہ جائز ہے۔ اور استثنیٰ کی مشابہت کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ چاروں صورتوں میں بیع فاسد ہو جائے کیونکہ اسنے غیر طبیع کو بیچ کے قبول کرنے کیلئے شرط نبادیا ہے اور یہ جائز نہیں ہے
احناف تطبیق کا راستہ اختیار کرتے ہوئے یہ کہتے ہیں کہ دونوں مشابہتوں کا لحاظ رکھا جائیگا۔ لہذا جب محل خیار اور شمن دونوں کا پتہ ہو جیسا کہ پہلی صورت میں ہے" تو ناسخ کی مشابہت کیوجہ سے بھی صحیح ہو جائے گی باقی تین صورتوں میں استثنیٰ کی مشابہت کی وجہ سے بیع فاسد ہو جا ئے گی اس لئے کہ جب بیچ اور ثمن دونوں مجہول ہو یا ان میں سے کوئی ایک مجہول ہو تو بیع فاسد ہو جاتی ہے اور ان صورتوں میں ناسخ کی مشابہت کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہوتا اس لئے کہ ناسخ مجہول خود ساقط ہو جاتا ہے ہے اس وجہ سے شرط خیار بھی باطل ہوگی اور دونوں غلاموں میں عقد بیع ثابت ہو گی حالانکہ یہ خلاف مقصود ہے۔
قوله : وقيل انه يسقط الاحتجاج به كالاستثنا المجهول لأن كل واحد منها لبيان انه لم يدخل فصار كالبيع
He said: So it becomes as if he sold two slaves for one thousand, on the condition that he has the option in one specified slave, and he named its price. [M?s?f?] compared [M?dh?b M?n?r?] to a fiqhi issue, and that issue is: A person bought two slaves for one thousand dirhams, and stipulated the option in one specific slave among them, and also mentioned its price separately. In fact, this issue has four forms: (1) The first form: The object of option is one specific slave among the two, and its price is also specified. (2) The second form: Neither is specified. (3) The third form: The object of option is specified but its price is not mentioned. (4) The fourth form: The price of that slave is mentioned but the object of option is not specified. Now, the system in which the option exists is included in the contract, and the price is not included in the ruling of sale. So from the aspect that it is included in the contract, returning the sale due to the condition of option is like changing the contract of sale—this becomes similarity to abrogation. And from the aspect that it is not included in the ruling, returning it is like stating that it was never included in the ruling—so this becomes similarity to exception. The similarity to abrogation requires that in all four forms the sale be valid, because these two slaves can form a complete sale; it should not be objected due to non-permissibility of sale by portion (bay' bil-hissah), because sale by portion initially is not permissible, and this is not an initial sale by portion but a continuation, and that is permissible. And the similarity to exception requires that in all four forms the sale be invalid, because he stipulated a condition for the acceptance of the sale that is not natural, and that is not permissible. The Hanafis, adopting the path of reconciliation (tathbīq), say that both similarities will be considered. Hence, when the object of option and the price are both known, as in the first form, then due to the similarity to abrogation, the sale will be valid. In the remaining three forms, due to the similarity to exception, the sale will be invalid, because when the sale and the price are both unknown, or one of them is unknown, the sale becomes invalid. And in those forms, the similarity to abrogation is not considered, because an unknown abrogator itself drops, so the condition of option becomes void and the contract of sale would be established for both slaves, whereas this is contrary to the intended purpose. He said: And it is said that the argument is dropped due to it, like an unknown exception, because each one of them is to explain that it did not enter, so it becomes like a sale... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage illustrates an usul principle through a fiqhi example. The author quotes the matn's statement comparing the issue to a sale of two slaves with an option in one. The commentator explains that this example has four forms, each differing in whether the object of option (maḥall al-khiyār) and its price (thaman) are specified. The reasoning hinges on two similarities: the similarity to nāsikh (abrogator) and the similarity to mustathnā (exception). If the option is considered part of the contract, revoking the sale due to option resembles abrogation (changing the contract). If the option is not part of the ruling, revoking it is like exception (clarifying that it was never included). The similarity to nāsikh would make the sale valid in all forms, because the remaining parts of the contract can stand without the option. The similarity to mustathnā would make the sale invalid, because the condition is not naturally part of the sale. The Hanafis reconcile by giving precedence to the similarity that fits the circumstances: when both the object of option and its price are known (first form), the nāsikh similarity applies, making the sale valid. When either is unknown (forms 2-4), the mustathnā similarity applies, making the sale invalid because ignorance of the condition's subject or price corrupts the contract. This is because an unknown abrogator (nāsikh majhūl) drops completely, which would incorrectly void the option yet keep the sale for both slaves, contrary to the stipulator's intent.

Key Terms
خیار (khiyār): Option; the right to rescind or confirm a contract within a specified period.
محل خیار (maḥall al-khiyār): The specific item or part of the contract that is subject to the option.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the monetary consideration in a sale.
بیع بالحصہ (bay' bil-ḥiṣṣah): Sale of a portion or share without specification; generally not permissible initially.
تشبیہ (tashbīh): Analogy; likening one thing to another for legal reasoning.
تطبیق (taṭbīq): Reconciliation; harmonizing conflicting evidences or analogies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of selling two slaves with option in one, which form of the transaction is considered valid (sahih) according to the Hanafis?
A) When neither the object of option nor its price is specified.
B) When the object of option is specified but its price is not mentioned.
C) When the price is specified but the object of option is not.
D) When both the object of option and its price are specified.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method reconciles conflicting analogies by conditioning the ruling on the presence or absence of knowledge, applying the naskh analogy when the condition is specified and the istithna analogy when it is not.

The Internal Hanafi Dispute on 'Amm after Takhsis
Presents two opposing views on the status of 'amm after particularization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the second and third Hanafi madhahib on the status of 'amm after takhsis and apply their respective analogies (istithna vs. naskh) to derive fiqhi rulings.

Core Idea
After partial specification, 'amm is either invalid for inference (like exception) or remains definitive (like abrogation).

This lesson explores a critical disagreement within the Hanafi school: what happens to a general text after some of its individuals are excluded? Two approaches emerge — one comparing it to exception (istithna), the other to abrogation (naskh). Understanding this dispute sharpens your usul analysis and reveals how methodological choices affect fiqhi outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the issue: status of 'amm after partial takhsis
Identify the problem: when a general text is partially specified, can the remaining generality still be used as a definitive proof? The second madhhab says it becomes invalid for istidlal; the third says it remains qat'i.
2
Second madhhab: invalidity by analogy to exception
Explain the view of Karkhi and 'Isa ibn Aban: the specifying evidence functions like an exception, removing the specified individuals from the scope, making the rest unreliable for inference.
Key: they treat takhsis like istithna, not like naskh.
3
Third madhhab: remaining definitive by analogy to abrogation
Explain the opposing view: takhsis is like naskh — each part is independent, so specification of some individuals does not weaken the generality for the others.
Key: they treat takhsis like naskh, emphasizing the independence of each specification.
4
Compare the fiqhi examples
Examine the example of selling a free man and a slave: second madhhab invalidates the whole sale (exception analogy), third madhhab allows sale of the slave if one dies (abrogation analogy). Also note the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the Two Imams on separate pricing.
The example illustrates the practical thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دوسرا مذہب: عام مخصوص البعض کے بارے میں دوسرا مذہب بیان کرتے ہیں اور وہ یہ ہے کہ اس سے استدلال کرنا ساقط ہے جیسے کہ [انی مجبول؟] میں ہوتا ہے اس لئے کہ اس میں سے ہر ایک اس بات کو بیان کرنے کیلئے ہے کہ یہ اپنے ماقبل کے حکم میں داخل نہیں ہے یہ امام ابوالحسن حنفی کرخی اور عیسی بن ابان رحمہم اللہ کا مذہب ہے یہ لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ مخصوص خواہ معلوم ہو یا مجہول ہو بہر دو صورت عام استدلال کے قابل نہیں رہے گا جیسا کہ اللہ تعالی کا حکم اقتلوا المشركين .. الخ ظاہر ہی بات ہے کہ اس عموم میں تمام مشترک بشمول اہل ذمہ داخل تھے مگر بعض وہ مشرک جو زمی ہیں خاص کر دیئے گئے اور فرمایا ولا تقتلوا اهل الذمة یہ خصوص مجہول کی مثال ہے اور مخصوص معلوم کی مثال اسی عموم کے بعد یہ فرمانا ہے ولا تقتلوا بعضهم یہ لوگ صرف یعنی [بہت؟] استثناء کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں اور صیغہ یعنی جہت نسخ کا اعتبار نہیں کرتے جیسا کہ جب حر اور غلام کو ایک ہی ثمن کے ساتھ فروخت کیا جائے تو چونکہ حر تو بیع میں داخل نہیں ہے لہذا وہ بیع میں سے مستثنی ہوگا چونکہ یہ بیع بالحصہ ابتدا ہے اور یہ باطل ہے۔ لہذا غلام میں بھی بیع درست نہ ہوگی البتہ اگر ہر ایک کی علیحدہ قیمت بیان کر دے تو امام اعظم رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک پھر بھی بیع فاسد ہی رہے گی اور صاحبین رحمہما اللہ کے ہاں جائز ہوگی۔ قوله: وقيل انه يبقى كما كان اعتبارا بالناسخ لأن كل واحد منهما مستقل بنفسه بخلاف الاستثناء فصار كما إذا باع عبدين وهلك أحدهما قبل التسليم. مذہب ثالث: عام مخصوص البعض کے بارے میں تیسرا مذہب یہ ہے کہ عام تخصیص کے بعد بھی ویسے ہی قطعی رہے گا جیسا کہ تخصیص سے پہلے تھا اس مذہب کے قائلین اس کو ناسخ پر قیاس کرتے ہیں۔ اس لئے کہ ان میں سے ہر ایک مستقل بنفسہ ہوتا ہے یہ صرف صیغے یعنی جہت نسخ کو دیکھتے ہیں اور معنی یعنی جہت استثناء کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں کرتے ہیں اس کی نظیر یہ مسئلہ فقہیہ ہے جیسا کہ اگر کسی نے دو غلام فروخت کئے اور ان کی علیحدہ علیحدہ قیمت بیان نہیں کی اور ان میں سے ایک غلام مشتری کے حوالے کرنے سے پہلے مرگیا تو گویا کہ اس کی بیع منسوخ ہوگئی اور جو زندہ ہے اس کی بیع باقی رہے گی اور چونکہ یہ بیع بالحصہ بقاء ہے لہذا جائز ہے۔
Second madhhab: Regarding a general text that has been partially particularized, they present the second madhhab, which is that inference from it is invalid, as is the case in [innate?], because each part is only to explain that it is not included in the ruling of what precedes it. This is the madhhab of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Hanafi al-Karkhi and 'Isa ibn Aban, may Allah have mercy on them. They say that the particularized [text], whether the specification is known or unknown, in both cases the general will not be suitable for inference. For example, Allah's command, 'Kill the polytheists...' it is obvious that this generality included all polytheists including the people of dhimmah, but some of the polytheists who are dhimmis were specified, and He said, 'Do not kill the people of dhimmah.' This is an example of unknown specification. And an example of known specification is, after this generality, His saying, 'Do not kill some of them.' These people only consider the form, i.e., the mode of exception, and do not consider the aspect of abrogation. For example, when a free man and a slave are sold with one price, since the free man is not included in the sale, therefore he will be excluded from the sale. Since this is a sale by portion from the beginning, it is invalid. Hence the sale of the slave will also not be valid. However, if he states a separate price for each, then according to Imam A'zam (Abu Hanifa), the sale will still be invalid, while according to the Two Imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it will be valid. His saying: And it is said that it remains as it was, considering [the principle of] abrogation, because each [act of specification] is independent in itself, unlike exception. So it becomes like when someone sells two slaves and one of them dies before delivery. Third madhhab: Regarding a general that has been partially particularized, the third madhhab is that the general, after specification, remains definitive just as it was before specification. The proponents of this madhhab analogize it to abrogation, because each is independent in itself. They only consider the wording, i.e., the mode of abrogation, and do not consider the meaning, i.e., the mode of exception. Its fiqhi parallel is the following case: If someone sold two slaves and did not state a separate price for each, and one of the slaves died before delivery to the buyer, then his sale is considered annulled, and the sale of the living one remains valid. And since this is a sale by portion in continuance, it is valid.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct positions within the Hanafi usul tradition regarding the status of a general text (عام) after it has been partially particularized (تخصیص البعض). The second madhhab, attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi and 'Isa ibn Aban, holds that after specification, the general can no longer be used as a proof (استدلال). They consider that the specifying evidence effectively removes the specified individuals from the scope of the general, and since the specification may be unknown in scope, the remaining generality is unreliable. Their example is the Quranic command 'Kill the polytheists' which initially included all polytheists, but was then specified by 'Do not kill the people of dhimmah' — an unknown specification since the exact category of 'people of dhimmah' is defined elsewhere. They treat this as a case of exception (استثناء), not abrogation (نسخ). In contrast, the third madhhab argues that the general remains definitive (قطعي) as before, drawing an analogy to abrogation (نسخ). They argue that each specifying evidence is independent, so specification does not affect the general's strength. Their fiqhi example: selling two slaves for one price, then one dies before delivery — by analogy to abrogation, the sale of the surviving slave remains valid as a sale by portion in continuance. The passage then quotes the matn which states the third view explicitly. The commentary highlights the key methodological difference: the second madhhab focuses on the form of exception (صیغة استثناء), while the third focuses on the form of abrogation (صیغة نسخ), leading to different conclusions about the general's usability.

Key Terms
عام (‘Amm): A word or phrase that includes all individuals of its category without restriction
تخصیص (Takhsis): The specification of a general term by excluding some of its individuals
استثناء (Istithna): Exception, a grammatical form that excludes something from a general statement, considered as not part of the general ruling
نسخ (Naskh): Abrogation, the removal of a ruling by a later revelation or evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second madhhab (Karkhi and 'Isa ibn Aban), what happens to a general text after partial specification?
A) It remains equally definitive as before.
B) It becomes invalid for inference (istidlal).
C) It becomes majazi (metaphorical).
D) It is considered abrogated wholly.

Key Takeaway
The internal Hanafi dispute over 'amm after takhsis hinges on whether specification functions like exception (istithna) or abrogation (naskh), leading to different fiqhi conclusions.
